

{0}------------------------------------------------

# Public-Key Quantum Fire and Key-Fire From Classical Oracles

Alper C¸ akan<sup>∗</sup> Vipul Goyal† Omri Shmueli‡

### Abstract

Quantum fire is a distribution of quantum states that can be efficiently cloned, but cannot be efficiently converted into a classical string. First considered by Nehoran and Zhandry (ITCS'24) and later formalized by Bostanci, Nehoran, Zhandry (STOC'25), quantum fire has strong applications and implications in cryptography, along with important connections to physics and complexity. However, constructing and proving the security of quantum fire so far has been elusive. Nehoran and Zhandry showed how to construct quantum fire relative to an inefficient quantum oracle (which cannot be instantiated even heuristically). Later, Bostanci, Nehoran, Zhandry gave a candidate construction based on group actions, however, even in the classical oracle model they could only conjecture the security of their scheme, and were not able to give a security proof or argue security.

In this work, for the first time, we give a construction of public-key quantum fire relative to a classical oracle and prove its security unconditionally. Going further, we introduce two stronger notions that generalize quantum fire, and we give secure constructions for these notions.

- We introduce a notion called quantum key-fire where the clonable fire states serve as keys: They can be used to evaluate a functionality (such as a signing or decryption key), and for security we require unbounded leakage-resilience, which means that given the fire state, the key cannot be efficiently converted into a classical string.
- We consider the notion of interactive (i.e. LOCC) security for quantum (key-)fire. In this setting, instead of trying to convert the state or key into a classical string; the adversary, given the flame state, attempts to transfer it to another adversary interactively over a classical channel.

We give a construction of quantum key-fire relative to a classical oracle and unconditionally prove that it satisfies interactive security for any unlearnable functionality. As a result, we also obtain the first classical oracle separations between various notions in physics and cryptography:

- A separation in the computational universe between two fundamental principles of quantum mechanics: No-cloning and no-teleportation, which are equivalent in the informationtheoretic setting.
- A separation between copy-protection security (Aaronson, CCC'09) and unbounded/LOCC leakage-resilience security (C¸ akan, Goyal, Liu-Zhang, Ribeiro, TCC'24).
- A separation between computational no-cloning security and computational no-learning security, two notions introduced recently by Fefferman, Ghosh, Sinha, Yuen (ITCS'26).

In all our constructions, the oracles can be implemented efficiently using one-way functions. Thus, our schemes can be heuristically instantiated in the plain model using one-way functions and indistinguishability obfuscation, which gives us the first constructions in the plain model with concrete evidence for their security.

Keywords: Quantum cryptography, leakage-resilience, quantum protection

<sup>∗</sup>Carnegie Mellon University. alpercakan98@gmail.com.

<sup>†</sup>NTT Research & Carnegie Mellon University. vipul@vipulgoyal.org

<sup>‡</sup>NTT Research. omri.shmueli1@gmail.com.

# <span id="page-3-0"></span>1 Introduction

Possibly the two most well-known basic principles of quantum mechanics are the so-called nocloning and the no-teleportation principles. The first principle says that no physical process can create two copies of an unknown quantum state when given a single copy, and the second principle (which we will refer to as the no-telegraphing principle[1](#page-3-1) ) says that no physical process, given a single copy of a quantum state, can convert it into a classical string from which the state can be reconstructed.

Apart from being fundamental rules of the quantum universe we live in, these principles have also found a tremendous amount of applications in quantum cryptography. The no-cloning principle has had profound applications, from being utilized to construct quantum money in the very first paper of [\[Wie83\]](#page-95-0) that started the field of quantum cryptography, to fundamental applications like quantum key-distribution and even more modern applications such as copy-protection (anti-piracy security) for software ([\[Aar09,](#page-93-0) [CLLZ21,](#page-94-0) [C¸ G25\]](#page-93-1)). The no-telegraphing principle has also found important applications in cryptography, such as the notion of unbounded/LOCC leakage-resilience ([\[C¸ GLZR24,](#page-94-1) [NZ24\]](#page-95-1)).

It is well-known in quantum information theory that the no-cloning and the no-telegraphing principles are equivalent: If one can telegraph a state then they can also clone it, and if one can clone a state they can also telegraph it by performing state tomography after creating a sufficient number of copies of the state. One caveat is that state tomography necessarily requires exponentially many copies in the number of qubits ([\[FGLE12,](#page-94-2) [OW16\]](#page-95-2)), and the resulting classical string (describing the state) will also be of exponential size. Thus, the equivalency between the two principles relies on an inefficient physical process. However, it is one of the fundamental hypotheses of quantum complexity theory that any physical process we can observe in real-life is quantum polynomial time. Thus, one might ask the following question: Are these two principles of quantum mechanics equivalent through physically-observable processes (or, reasonable-size experiments in the terminology of [\[BBJ16\]](#page-93-2))? Indeed, Nehoran and Zhandry ([\[NZ24\]](#page-95-1), ITCS'24) precisely asked this question and showed that the answer is likely negative, by showing a separation relative to an inefficient quantum oracle. While this is a step towards answering the question, unfortunately, quantum oracle separations might be misleading ([\[AK25\]](#page-93-3)) and in fact it is even unclear how exactly to model them ([\[Zha25\]](#page-96-0)). Later, Bostanci, Nehoran, Zhandry ([\[BNZ25\]](#page-93-4), STOC'25) gave a candidate classical oracle separation based on cryptographic group actions; however, they were not able to give a proof or argue security. Thus, what we know so far about the relationship between these two principles in a computational universe is unsatisfactory, and thus we ask the following question:

Can we show a separation between the no-cloning and the no-telegraphing principles in a computational universe?

Note that this question has fundamental implications for our understanding of quantum states, as well as to cryptography and complexity (as we explain in detail in the subsequent sections). It is widely believed that quantum states are more powerful than classical strings (e.g. the wellknown QMA vs QCMA question). However, what exactly is the fundamental property of quantum states that makes them more powerful than classical strings? Is it unclonability? If we show a clonable state that satisfies the no-telegraphing principle, then the answer is negative: Even clonable quantum states are more powerful then classical strings.

<span id="page-3-1"></span><sup>1</sup>We follow this naming convention that [\[NZ24\]](#page-95-1) introduced to avoid confusion with quantum teleportation

{4}------------------------------------------------

Cryptographic Applications. [\[BNZ25\]](#page-93-4) defined a notion called quantum fire, which can be thought of a cryptographic version of the aforementioned separation[2](#page-4-1) . In more detail, quantum fire is defined to be a distribution of quantum states (called flame states) which can be efficiently cloned. For security, we require untelegraphability: No efficient adversary who is given a flame state can convert it into a classical string from which the state can be reconstructed. [\[BNZ25\]](#page-93-4) mention possible applications of quantum fire, such as storing some information in a quantum encoding (i.e. as a flame state) on a server to protect it from exfiltration by adversaries. However, the state of affairs for existence of quantum fire is unsatisfactory: Just like above, the only known constructions are the inefficient quantum oracle based construction of [\[NZ24\]](#page-95-1) and the candidate construction of [\[BNZ25\]](#page-93-4) without a proof of security. Thus, we ask the following question.

Can we construct quantum fire with provable security?

Given the myriad of applications of the no-cloning and the no-telegraphing notions in cryptography, we also ask whether a separation between them also has applications in cryptography, beyond quantum fire.

Does a separation between no-cloning and no-telegraphing have applications in cryptography?

## <span id="page-4-0"></span>1.1 Our Results

In this work, we answer the above questions in the classical oracle model.

Quantum Fire We give a construction of quantum fire relative to a quantumly-accessible[3](#page-4-2) classical oracle and prove its correctness and security unconditionally, for the first time.

Theorem 1 (Informal). Relative to a classical oracle, there exists a public-key quantum fire scheme with untelegraphability security.

This also gives the first classical oracle separation between no-cloning and no-telegraphing and resolves the open questions posed by [\[NZ24\]](#page-95-1) and [\[BNZ25\]](#page-93-4). We note that our construction is completely different from previous works.

Our scheme can be implemented efficiently using one-way functions, and thus can be instantiated heuristically using indistinguishability obfuscation to obtain the first quantum fire scheme in the plain model with concrete evidence for its security.

This significantly improves upon existing results. As discussed above, previously [\[NZ24\]](#page-95-1) gave a construction of quantum fire relative to an inefficient unitary quantum oracle (which bakes cloning into the oracle itself and thus it is inherently inefficient), and [\[BNZ25\]](#page-93-4) gave a candidate construction based on cryptographic group actions. However, even in the classical oracle model, [\[BNZ25\]](#page-93-4) only conjectured security and were not able to prove security. In fact, they even claimed that any quantum fire scheme with proof of security in the classical oracle model would likely lead to a classical oracle separation between QMA and QCMA[4](#page-4-3) , which is one of the most biggest questions in quantum complexity that has been open for almost 20 years[5](#page-4-4) .

<span id="page-4-2"></span><span id="page-4-1"></span><sup>2</sup> Indeed, quantum fire is stronger than the separation, as it immediately implies the separation.

<sup>3</sup>Throughout the paper, we will write classical oracle for brevity, and we always mean quantumly-accessible classical oracle.

<span id="page-4-3"></span><sup>4</sup>We note that our construction does not yield a separation between QMA and QCMA. However, it is an interesting question if our techniques could be useful.

<span id="page-4-4"></span><sup>5</sup>Subsequent to our work, a new work by Bostanci, Haferkamp, Nirkhe, Zhandry (STOC'26) solved this problem via a different approach.

{5}------------------------------------------------

Stronger Notions Going further, we introduce two stronger notions that generalize quantum fire.

- First, we introduce quantum key-fire, where the fire states encode a key that can be used to evaluate a functionality (e.g. a signing key, or a decryption key, or a software). For quantum key-fire, as the security notion, instead of untelegraphability, we require the stronger guarantee of unbounded leakage-resilience ([\[C¸ GLZR24\]](#page-94-1)). Unbounded leakage-resilience is a strengthening of no-telegraphing where not only that the flame state cannot be encoded into a classical string, we further require that no efficiently generated classical string (called leakage in this context) can help evaluate the functionality or win a security game related to the functionality. In other words, quantum key-fire is to quantum fire what quantum copyprotection ([\[Aar09,](#page-93-0) [ALL](#page-93-5)+21, [CLLZ21\]](#page-94-0)) is to public-key quantum money, and quantum-key fire implies quantum fire in a straightforward way.
- As the second generalization, we consider the notion of interactive security. Previous work only considered the single message untelegraphability security game where the adversary A<sup>1</sup> who has the flame state (the sender adversary) produces a classical string, using which the second adversary A<sup>2</sup> (the receiver adversary) tries to reconstruct the flame state. We consider the case where the sender and the receiver interact over a classical channel for any (polynomial) number of rounds. We call this notion interactive untelegraphability security in the case of quantum fire, and local operations and classical communication (LOCC) leakageresilience ([\[C¸ GLZR24\]](#page-94-1)) in the case of quantum key-fire.

To recap, we have four security notions for quantum fire and key-fire: Untelegraphability, interactive untelegraphability, unbounded leakage-resilience and LOCC leakageresilience - a summary is given in [Table 1.](#page-5-0) Note that LOCC leakage-resilience implies interactive untelegraphability, and unbounded leakage-resilience implies untelegraphability.

<span id="page-5-0"></span>

| Security Notion                                   | A1<br>Interaction<br>Between<br>and<br>A2 | A2<br>Goal of                                              |
|---------------------------------------------------|-------------------------------------------|------------------------------------------------------------|
| Untelegraphability                                | No                                        | Reconstruct<br>the<br>exact<br>same<br>state               |
| Interactive Untelegraphability                    | Arbitrary classical interaction           | Reconstruct<br>the<br>exact<br>same<br>state               |
| Unbounded<br>Leakage<br>Re<br>silience            | No                                        | Solve a challenge (e.g. decrypt<br>a challenge ciphertext) |
| LOCC Leakage Resilience (the<br>strongest notion) | Arbitrary classical interaction           | Solve a challenge (e.g. decrypt<br>a challenge ciphertext) |

Table 1: Comparison of security notions for quantum fire and key-fire.

Quantum Key-Fire We give a construction of quantum key-fire relative to a classical oracle and prove that it satisfies LOCC leakage-resilience for all unlearnable functionalities.

Theorem 2 (Informal). Relative to a classical oracle, there exists a quantum key-fire scheme that satisfies LOCC leakage-resilience for any unlearnable functionality.

{6}------------------------------------------------

We note that this is the best possible result since even in the oracle model, unbounded/LOCC leakage-resilience for learnable functionalities is trivially impossible.

As above, our scheme can be instantiated efficiently using one-way functions and indistinguishability obfuscation in the plain model with heuristic security.

Previously, even in the quantum oracle model, only provably secure construction of quantum fire [\[NZ24\]](#page-95-1) had proof of security in the significantly weaker setting of single-message untelegraphability and there was no proof of even 2-message interactive security.

Size of the Leakage Finally, we note that, unlike previous work, we impose no restriction on the length of the messages between the adversaries (e.g. they can be doubly exponential in the security parameter, or more): The only requirement for security is that the adversaries make polynomially many queries to the oracles.

Applications As we will discuss later in more detail (see [Section 1.2\)](#page-7-0), quantum key-fire is a strong primitive in the setting of leakage-resilient cryptography. It retains the security of the functionality (e.g. a decryption key) even in the face of unbounded leakages, which is impossible with classical keys, but at the same time (assuming one has a quantum computer) it is almost as practical as a classical key due to its clonability: It can be later on used in different locations/servers, can be kept alive for long periods by frequently refreshing/cloning it (almost like a classical dynamic RAM) and so on.

Implications As we discuss in [Section 1.2,](#page-7-0) our results have interesting implications in cryptography, physics, and complexity, and we believe it also leads the way for a myriad of interesting new research directions (such as clonable quantum cryptography) and questions.

As an example of cryptographic implications, our results imply a separation between the recently introduced cryptographic assumptions of computational no-cloning assumption and computational no-learning assumption, thus answering an open question of [\[FGSY25\]](#page-94-3). Our results also imply a separation between the security notions of copy-protection security and unbounded/LOCC leakageresilience security, answering the open questions of [\[NZ24\]](#page-95-1) and [\[C¸ GLZR24\]](#page-94-1). As an example of physical implications, our result shows that the fundamental property that makes quantum states more powerful than classical strings is not unclonability (since even clonable states cannot be converted into classical strings).

Technical Contributions Our work introduces new techniques in the oracle model for analyzing two quantum adversaries that are communicating interactively for arbitrary number of rounds over a classical channel (i.e. LOCC adversaries), which are in general hard to analyze due to internal unclonable states of the adversaries. Through our new techniques, we also draw interesting connections between LOCC adversaries and a natural property called incompressibility of oracles that says that not too many outputs of the oracle can be packed inside a classical string (see [Sections 6](#page-35-1) and [7](#page-46-0) for the formal definitions). We also prove that our incompressibility notion is satisfied for two particular cases: for random oracles and for a modified version of the one-shot signature scheme of [\[SZ25\]](#page-95-3).

We believe our new techniques and our new incompressibility results might be helpful for other problems in similar settings.

Theorem 3 (Informal). Random oracles satisfy classical-message incompressibility.

Theorem 4 (Informal). Relative to a classical oracle, there exists a one-shot signature scheme with incompressibility.

{7}------------------------------------------------

### <span id="page-7-0"></span>1.2 Applications, Implications and Future Directions

In this section, we will discuss the cryptographic applications (e.g. constructions) of our results, and then discuss the implications of our results (e.g. separations we obtain) and finally discuss some new research directions motivated by our results.

We first recall the notion of leakage-resilience.

Primer on Leakage-Resilience Storing secret keys (e.g. of an encryption scheme or a signature scheme) is one of the oldest and most fundamental problems in cryptography. Leakage-resilient cryptography is a fruitful research area in cryptography that has received significant interest over decades that aims to deal with this problem: It aims to construct schemes which are secure in face of attacks where adversary obtains leakage on the honest parties secret keys, through side-channels [\[Koc96,](#page-94-4) [QS01,](#page-95-4) [AARR03\]](#page-93-6). Side-channels are generally various measurements of the system storing the keys, such as the electromagnetic radiation emitted, or the power consumed during computation.

In classical leakage-resilient cryptography, the leakage output is modeled as as function f(sk) of the secret key sk where f has an output size significantly smaller than the size of sk. This is a somewhat artificial restriction that is nevertheless ubiquitous, since otherwise there is no possibility of obtaining security guarantees: the adversary can simply obtain the whole secret key.

The recent work of [\[C¸ GLZR24\]](#page-94-1) has introduced[6](#page-7-1) the notion of unbounded leakage-resilience, where the secret key is stored as a quantum key, and the leakage measurement output is allowed to be of any unbounded size[7](#page-7-2) , in particular, it can be much larger than the size of the secret key! They also introduced the notion of LOCC leakage-resilience where the adversary adaptively obtains unbounded leakage over any number of rounds. They showed, through a new proof, that the unclonable[8](#page-7-3) public-key encryption and signing schemes of [\[CLLZ21\]](#page-94-0) also satisfy LOCC leakageresilience, thus giving a plain model construction assuming indistinguishability obfuscation (iO) and one-way functions.

Application: Unbounded/LOCC Leakage-Resilience with Clonable Keys The notion of unbounded/LOCC leakage-resilience is a strong (and uniquely quantum) security guarantee with important practical ramifications. However, a major issue with the existing scheme of [\[C¸ GLZR24\]](#page-94-1) is that while the secret quantum key satisfies unbounded/LOCC leakage resilience, the key is also unclonable. This severely limits the practicality of the scheme, since if we ever need to use the decryption key in multiple systems, this will not be possible[9](#page-7-4) . Further, while there have been significant advances in quantum computation in recent years, quantum memory implementations can still keep states coherent for very short amounts of time.

However, a quantum key-fire scheme gives us the best of both worlds: While the keys satisfy LOCC leakage-resilience (which is impossible classically), they are also efficiently clonable, which makes it almost as useful as a classical key! For example, the key can be used in different computers too (e.g. to parallelize big tasks, or if these are the decryption keys for a company and a new employee joins). Further, they can be frequently cloned in the quantum memory to refresh them to keep them alive for much longer and prevent decoherence, much like a classical dynamic RAM

<span id="page-7-1"></span><sup>6</sup>Concurrent to [\[C¸ GLZR24\]](#page-94-1), [\[NZ24\]](#page-95-1) introduced exfiltration security, which is essentially equivalent to noninteractive unbounded leakage-resilience. They did not give a plain model construction from standard cryptographic assumptions.

<span id="page-7-2"></span><sup>7</sup>The only requirements is that leakage is a measurement, i.e., it has a classical output. However, measurements accurately model side-channel attacks and in fact capture all known attacks.

<span id="page-7-4"></span><span id="page-7-3"></span><sup>8</sup>That is, the decryption/signing keys of the schemes satisfy copy-protection security

<sup>9</sup>Naturally, we cannot store a classical description of the key either, since that would defeat the whole purpose and we would lose the unbounded leakage-resilience guarantee.

{8}------------------------------------------------

that are widely used in practice. Thus, we believe quantum key-fire will have important practical ramifications in the future.

Separating Leakage-Resilience and Copy-Protection Related to above discussion, our quantum key-fire result also gives a separation between the security notions of unbounded/LOCC leakage-resilience ([\[C¸ GLZR24\]](#page-94-1)) and copy-protection ([\[Aar09\]](#page-93-0)), answering an open question of [\[C¸ GLZR24,](#page-94-1) [NZ24\]](#page-95-1). Note that so far, only known unbounded/LOCC leakage-resilient schemes also satisfy copy-protection security. Copy-protected schemes are known to imply public-key quantum money (PKQM), and constructing PKQM without obfuscation is one of the biggest problems in quantum cryptography. However, our work for the first time shows that leakage-resilience does not imply copy-protection/unclonability, which means there might be a hope of achieving unbounded/LOCC leakage-resilience without obfuscation. On the (slightly) negative side, our separation shows that we cannot simply prove unbounded/LOCC leakage-resilience of a scheme and be content that it also satisfies copy-protection security: when the goal is to obtain copy-protection security, we need to prove it separately.

Clonable Cryptography and Separations in MicroCrypt MicroCrypt is the study of existence of quantum cryptographic primitives (such as quantum commitments, one-way quantum-state generators etc) even in a world where P = NP, and it has gained significant attention in the recent years. Recent work of [\[FGSY25\]](#page-94-3) introduced two new quantum cryptographic assumptions for MicroCrypt: the computational no-learning assumption and the computational no-cloning assumption, and they show how to build various MicroCrypt primitives from these assumptions. They also conjecture that the computational no-learning assumption is potentially weaker (i.e., it is a more conservative assumption). Our work shows that relative to a classical oracle, there exists a family of circuits for which the computational no-learning holds, but the computational no-cloning assumption does not. This resolves their open question. We believe our techniques might also help separate various MicroCrypt primitives, such as one-way state generators and unclonable state generators.

We believe our work will also lead the way for a new research direction: clonable quantum cryptography. Our work shows that even clonable states are more powerful than classical strings. Thus, in any context where the purpose of using quantum states is not unclonability itself, it might be possible to obtain a clonable version of that quantum primitive, which would make it more practical (for example, the state can be kept alive for longer by refreshing it through cloning it, much like a classical dynamic RAM). For example, there are various works [\[GMMY24,](#page-94-5) [MY22,](#page-94-6) [KMNY24\]](#page-94-7) that show that various primitives (such as public-key encryption, signatures) can exist with quantum keys or quantum ciphertexts/signatures even if their classical-string counterparts do not exist. However, all such candidates so far have unclonable keys/signatures/ciphertexts, which significantly limits their practicality. Thus, it is an interesting question if one can show existence of clonable versions of these primitives in a world their classical counterpart do not exist, and we believe our techniques might help.

Physics in a Computational Universe In quantum mechanics, various fundamental principles called no-go theorems exist, such as the no-cloning theorem, no-hiding theorem, no-deleting theorem, no-telegraphing theorem and so on. Most of these no-go theorems have been shown to be equivalent to each other in the information-theoretic setting. However, our result gives the first (classical oracle) separation between the no-go principles of quantum mechanics. Our result means that, relative to a classical oracle, no-telegraphing does not imply no-cloning. Our result means that the 

{9}------------------------------------------------

equivalence of the fundamental no-go principles of quantum mechanics that we take for granted might not hold in a computational universe. An interesting question is if other principles of quantum mechanics can be separated in a computational universe.

Another interpretation of our result is as follows. In an information-theoretic setting, the main difference between a (local) quantum state/memory and a classical string is the unclonability of the quantum state, and clonable states can simply be turned into classical strings: We can clone it and apply a state tomography to obtain a classical description of it, and then work with this classical string. However, our work shows that in a computational world, even clonable states are different from classical memory, thus the fundamental property that distinguishes quantum states from classical memory is not unclonability.

Complexity Separating the complexity classes QMA and QCMA relative to a classical oracle is one of the most important questions in quantum cryptography and has been open since 2007. Due to the connection established by [\[NZ24\]](#page-95-1) between quantum fire and separating clonableQMA from QCMA (which also separates QMA and QCMA), [\[BNZ25\]](#page-93-4) claimed that a quantum fire scheme with proof of security in the classical oracle model would likely lead to a classical oracle separation between QMA and QCMA, solving this major open question. While our construction does not lead to such a separation, it is an interesting question if our techniques could help[10](#page-9-1) .

### <span id="page-9-0"></span>1.3 Organization

In [Section 2,](#page-10-0) we give a technical overview of the paper.

In [Section 4,](#page-25-1) we recall some prior definitions, such as quantum protection schemes and LOCC leakage-resilience security, that are relevant to defining and constructing quantum fire. In [Section 5,](#page-29-0) we give the quantum fire and quantum key-fire definitions.

In [Section 7,](#page-46-0) we prove an incompressibility result for random oracles. In [Section 6,](#page-35-1) we define and construct incompressible one-shot signatures.

In [Section 9,](#page-53-0) we give our main construction: quantum key-fire for signing. Then, we prove its verification and cloning correctness.

In [Section 10,](#page-63-0) we prove the security of our main construction, except for one major hybrid, which we prove in [Section 11](#page-85-0) for ease of reading.

{10}------------------------------------------------

## <span id="page-10-0"></span>2 Technical Overview

In this section, we give an overview of our work, and the end of the of the section we discuss some related work. Formal definitions, constructions and proofs can be found in relevant sections (see Section 1.3 for the organization). Note that for most of the overview, for pedagogical reasons, we will consider the notion of quantum fire mini-schemes with untelegraphability security. However, in the main body of the paper, we instead construct quantum key-fire and prove LOCC leakage-resilience security, which is stronger.

**Notation** We follow the common math/cryptography notation - more details can be found in Section 3.1, but importantly, we write R to denote quantum registers, and variables written in lower-case letters will be classical objects, such as sk, pk. Further, efficient adversary means quantum polynomial time (QPT) in the plain model and in the oracle model it means an adversary who makes polynomially many quantum queries to the oracles (it can take unbounded time outside oracle calls). We write  $\mathcal{O}$  to denote a classical oracle and write  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathcal{O}}$  or  $\mathcal{B}^{\mathcal{O}}$  to denote an adversary or algorithm with quantum query access to  $\mathcal{O}$ . Throughout the paper, oracle will always mean quantumly-accessible classical oracle (even when do not explicitly write so).

### <span id="page-10-1"></span>2.1 Definitions

We first start by providing an overview of the primitives and security definitions we consider in this overview - the formal definitions can be found in Section 5. The definition of one-shot signatures (which we use as a building block) can be found in Definition 9.

Quantum Fire Mini-Scheme In the first parts of this overview, we will work with a notion called quantum fire mini-scheme (Definition 14), which consists of three efficient quantum algorithms: Spark, Ver, Clone. QFireMini.Spark(1 $^{\lambda}$ ) outputs a serial number  $sn^*$  and a flame state  $|\psi_{sn^*}\rangle$ . QFire.Ver(sn,R) verifies if the state in the register R is a valid flame state for the serial number sn. Finally, QFire.Clone $(sn,R_{\text{flame}})$  outputs a register  $R_{\text{clone}}$ , which contains a copy of the the flame state in the register  $R_{\text{flame}}$ , along with outputting the register  $R_{\text{flame}}$  back. We require verification correctness and cloning correctness. Verification correctness requires that honestly generated states pass verification of Ver with overwhelming probability. For cloning correctness, we require that the Clone $(sn, |\psi_{sn}\rangle)$  outputs  $|\psi_{sn}\rangle \otimes |\psi_{sn}\rangle$ .

Untelegraphability Security In this overview, we will consider untelegraphability security (Definitions 13 and 15), which is defined by the following security game between a challenger and a pair of efficient adversaries  $\mathcal{A} = (\mathcal{A}_1, \mathcal{A}_2)$ . The sender adversary  $\mathcal{A}_1$  is given  $sn^*, |\psi_{sn^*}\rangle \leftarrow \mathsf{QFireMini.Spark}(1^{\lambda})$  and produces a classical output L. Afterwards, the receiver adversary  $\mathcal{A}_2(L)$  simply outputs a register R', which is tested for  $sn^*$  by running  $\mathsf{QFireMini.Ver}(sn^*, \mathsf{R'})$ . A scheme is said to satisfy untelegraphability security if for any efficient adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , the probability of winning the game is negligibly small.

Full Schemes In a full fledged quantum fire scheme QFire (Definition 11), there is a secret key sk with which QFire.Spark is run. In the security game (Definition 13), the adversary  $\mathcal{A}_1$  receives multiple independent flame states, and  $\mathcal{A}_2$  tries to produce a flame state with any serial number (not necessarily one received by  $\mathcal{A}_1$ ). Working with mini-schemes simplifies our discussion, and any mini-scheme can be generically upgraded to a secure full-fledged quantum fire scheme by using

{11}------------------------------------------------

a digital signature on the serial number sn: QFire.Spark(sk) outputs Sign(sk, sn)||sn, Rflame where sn, Rflame ← QFireMini.Spark(1<sup>λ</sup> ).

### <span id="page-11-0"></span>2.2 Construction

We now give an overview of our quantum fire mini-scheme construction, slowly building it to highlight the main ideas and design choices. The full version of our main construction (a key-fire scheme for signing) be found in [Section 9.](#page-53-0)

Intuitively, the notions of clonability and untelegraphability are at odds. The parties need to be able create new copies of the flame state |ψsn⟩, however, since we have classical oracles, we cannot simply output new copies of the state directly, or output any quantum states for that matter. Since we need to satisfy untelegraphability, we cannot output a description of the state directly either. Indeed, it is overall unclear how a classical oracle can help synthesize a state without simply outputting the description of the state in some form.

Starting Point As a starting point, we turn our attention to one-shot signature (OSS) schemes [\(Definitions 9](#page-27-1) and [10\)](#page-27-2), which has been recently constructed in the classical oracle model by [\[SZ25\]](#page-95-3). In a one-shot signature scheme, using a classical oracle O, parties are able to sample a single copy of a quantum state (called signing keys) |ϕvk⟩ for some random verification key vk, by running the algorithm OSSO.GenQKey(1<sup>λ</sup> ). Then, they can sign any message m ∈ {0, 1} using |ϕvk⟩, but the security requirement is that they cannot sign both 0 and 1 with the same vk value. In particular, this requires that |ϕvk⟩ cannot be cloned. Using OSS for quantum fire might seem counterintuitive: After all, the states |ϕvk⟩ are unclonable, which is the opposite of clonability(!), and a main application of OSS is classically-transferable quantum primitives (such as quantum money that can be transferred over classical channels), whereas untelegraphability is the polar opposite of this!

Indeed, while the focus of OSS schemes is unclonability of the states |ϕvk⟩, we take a new viewpoint: We observe that the OSS security also guarantees that parties can sample |ϕvk⟩ without learning its classical description! Thus, we consider using a one-shot signature signing key |ϕvk⟩ as our flame states, and we hope to use GenQKey as our cloning algorithm. However, there are two major issues with this approach. First, the state generation algorithm GenQKey outputs a different state |ϕvk′⟩ each time (necessarily, since otherwise OSS security would be broken) rather than outputting a copy of our state! While we solve this problem by using the purified output |η⟩ of OSSO.GenQKey(1<sup>λ</sup> ) (which is essentially a superposition P vk |vk⟩ |ϕvk⟩ of all possible keys), the second issue is much more significant: OSS.GenQKey is a public algorithm, and thus anyone can create |η⟩! Thus, the sender adversary A<sup>O</sup> 1 for quantum fire will not even need to prepare a classical encoding, since A<sup>O</sup> 2 can simply recreate the public state |η⟩ on its own, even though neither actually knows a classical encoding of it. To solve this issue, one might consider defining a secret key version of one-shot signatures (e.g. OSS.GenQKey requires sk), however, this would be of no help: (i) the secret key itself would serve as a classical encoding from which A<sup>2</sup> can recreate |η⟩, and more importantly (ii) the flame state must be publicly clonable, thus, we would need a new way to generate states without revealing the secret key, taking us back to square one.

Self-Encrypted Oracles To overcome these issues, we consider encrypting the oracle OOSS of OSS into a new oracle O′ in a way so that the oracle can be evaluated only if one proves that they already have the flame state |η⟩ (we will call this a |η⟩-verification mechanism). With this approach, we would at least eliminate the trivial attack where the receiver adversary A<sup>2</sup> can (without any message from A1) create |η⟩ on their own. Further, (publicly) cloning would still be possible, since 

{12}------------------------------------------------

(hopefully) one would be able to simulate a run of OSS.GenQKey $^{\mathcal{O}_{OSS}}(1^{\lambda})$  by decrypting outputs of  $\mathcal{O}'$  using their initial state  $|\eta\rangle$  and passing them along to OSS.GenQKey.

However, there are still major challenges with this approach. First, it is not immediately clear how one can prove to a classical oracle that they posses a quantum state. Second, even if one can achieve a sound oracle encryption, the mechanism used to achieve this and implement the encrypted oracle  $\mathcal{O}'$  must be so that we can actually decrypt and evaluate the original oracle  $\mathcal{O}_{\text{OSS}}$  correctly even for quantum queries, since OSS.GenQKey will have quantum queries to the oracle  $\mathcal{O}_{\text{OSS}}$ . Further, the  $|\eta\rangle$ -verification mechanism of our encrypted oracle will need to use (in some form) the verification mechanism of OSS itself (such as OSS.Ver), however, these algorithms also need access to  $\mathcal{O}_{\text{OSS}}$ , thus we will need to leave some parts of the oracle unencrypted without revealing too much information. Third, once we try to prove security, we run into a major circularity issue: While we want to say that the receiver adversary  $\mathcal{A}_2$  will not be able to create the state  $|\eta\rangle$  since it cannot decrypt the oracle  $\mathcal{O}'$ ; to show that  $\mathcal{A}_2$  cannot decrypt  $\mathcal{O}'$ , (since this oracle is encrypted under  $|\eta\rangle$ ) we need to show that it cannot have  $|\eta\rangle$  to begin with!

Our Oracles We now give an overview of our oracle encryption mechanism that solves all of these issues. We first note that our oracle encryption mechanism will not fully hide the oracle  $\mathcal{O}_{OSS}$  from  $\mathcal{A}_2$ : It will be able to evaluate it, but we will show that it can only do so at few points, which will be sufficient to prove that it cannot produce  $|\eta\rangle$ ,

Now we move onto our oracles. First, we require that the OSS scheme's oracle  $\mathcal{O}_{OSS}$  consists of three parts:  $\mathcal{O}_{OSS,Ver}$ ,  $\mathcal{O}_{OSS,Sign}$  and  $\mathcal{O}_{OSS,GenQKey}$  where each of the three algorithms (OSS,Ver, OSS,Sign, OSS,GenQKey) uses only the corresponding oracle. Of course, setting all of these oracles to be the full oracle of the scheme would also satisfy this, but as discussed, we require a non-trivial splitting of the oracle so that (i)  $\mathcal{O}_{OSS,Ver}$ ,  $\mathcal{O}_{OSS,Sign}$  do not reveal too much information (more details when we discuss the proof) and (ii)  $\mathcal{O}_{OSS,Ver}$ ,  $\mathcal{O}_{OSS,Sign}$  are sufficient for our  $|\eta\rangle$ -verification mechanism. Then, we define the following three oracles  $\mathcal{O}_0$ ,  $\mathcal{O}_1$ ,  $\mathcal{O}_2$  as our encrypted oracle  $\mathcal{O}'$ . On input vk, isig, z,  $\mathcal{O}_b$  (for  $b \in \{0,1\}$ ) verifies (using  $\mathcal{O}_{OSS,Ver}$ ) that isig is as valid OSS signature for the message b with verification key vk, and then it outputs  $H_b(vk||z)$  where  $H_0$ ,  $H_1$  are random functions. We will call the values  $H_b(vk||z)$  attestation strings. Finally, on input vk,  $y_0$ ,  $y_1$ , z,  $\mathcal{O}_2$  verifies that  $y_0 = H_0(vk||z)$ ,  $y_1 = H_1(vk||z)$ , and then outputs  $\mathcal{O}_{OSS,GenQKey}(z)$ .

Essentially, our encrypted oracle verifies that the party has a signature for both 0 and 1 (by verifying the corresponding attestation strings) with the same verification key, and then releases the output of the original oracle  $\mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{OSS.GenQKey}}(z)$ . Later, we will argue that this is indeed a sound  $|\eta\rangle$ -verification mechanism.

Design Choices: What Does Not Work Here, we motivate our oracle design given above by mentioning some other possible choices that do not work. First, note that using two separate oracles  $\mathcal{O}_0$ ,  $\mathcal{O}_1$  is required: We cannot have a single oracle that asks for a signature of 0 and 1 with the same verification key, since by the security of the one-shot signature scheme, we would not be able to unlock such an oracle encryption. Also note that it would not be sound to accept a signature of 0 and 1 with different verification keys because it leads to a simple attack: After cloning  $|\eta\rangle$  to get  $|\eta\rangle\otimes|\eta\rangle$ , the adversary  $\mathcal{A}_1$  can measure the verification key registers to obtain  $|\phi_{vk_1}\rangle$ ,  $|\phi_{vk_2}\rangle$ , and sign 0 and 1 with these keys respectively, and send the classical signatures to the receiver adversary  $\mathcal{A}_2$ . If we were to accept different verification keys in  $\mathcal{O}_2$ , the adversary  $\mathcal{A}_2$  would be able to run the oracle  $\mathcal{O}_2$  on any input z!

{13}------------------------------------------------

Cloning Now, we discuss cloning. To clone our flame state |η⟩ = P vk |vk⟩ ⊗ |ϕvk⟩, we simulate OSS.GenQKey while simulating its oracle queries using O0, O1, O2. Suppose OSS.GenQKey makes the quantum query |τ ⟩ = Pα<sup>z</sup> |z⟩, which would normally be executed with OGenQKey, but we simulate it as follows. We first run (a purified version) of OSS.Sign on |η⟩ to convert it to a superposition |η0⟩ = P vk <sup>|</sup>vk⟩ ⊗ P sig∈SIG(vk,0) |sig⟩ of signatures for the message 0. Then, we query O<sup>0</sup> on this state, which converts it to |η ′ 0 ⟩ = P z,vk <sup>α</sup><sup>z</sup> <sup>|</sup>z⟩ |vk⟩⊗P sig∈SIG(vk,0) |sig⟩ sig ⊗ |H0(vk||z)⟩ out<sup>0</sup> by definition of O0. Note that this can be also written as |η ′ 0 ⟩ = P z,vk <sup>α</sup><sup>z</sup> <sup>|</sup>z⟩ |vk⟩⊗P sig∈SIG(vk,0) |sig⟩ ⊗ |H0(vk||z)⟩ out<sup>0</sup> since the attestation string H0(vk||z) in the register out<sup>0</sup> does not depend on the particular signature sig on the signature register sig. Now, since we have isolated output the attestation string, we can actually undo (by applying the inverse unitary) the signing operation to get our original key back |η⟩! Then, we can similarly sign 1 and query O<sup>1</sup> to get the second attestation and end up with |η ′′⟩ = P z,vk <sup>α</sup><sup>z</sup> <sup>|</sup>z⟩ |vk⟩ ⊗ P sig∈SIG(vk,1) |sig⟩ ⊗ |H0(vk||z)⟩ out<sup>0</sup> ⊗ |H1(vk||z)⟩ out<sup>1</sup> . Finally, we can query O<sup>2</sup> with both our attestations in place to get output of the actual oracle OGenQKey. Afterwards, we show that we can again undo the signature to get our original key back, and be ready for decryption of the next query of OGenQKey.

More Design Choices We note that above, for everything to work smoothly, it is crucial that (i) our attestation strings do not depend on the particular signature (i.e. it is H0(vk||z) and not H0(vk||isig||z)) and (ii) we simply sign 0 and 1 rather than signing z (which would create an entanglement between the oracle input/output and our flame state because of the quantum queries of GenQKey, so we would not be able move onto the second query with a clean flame state). Here, we only (informally) discussed how to handle the first query - the full proof of cloning correctness requires carefully formalizing the above arguments and is given in [Section 9.4.](#page-58-1) We also note that, due to the design choice above necessitated by cloning correctness, as mentioned before our oracle encryption O0, O1, O<sup>2</sup> does not actually fully hide OOSS.GenQKey from A2. For example, A<sup>1</sup> can actually obtain (for some vk) H0(vk||z), H1(vk||z) for a list of z values, and send these to A<sup>2</sup> with which A<sup>2</sup> can obtain OOSS.GenQKey(z). However, we will show that this can happen only for a few points, and we will still be able to prove security.

Construction Summary To summarize, the oracles of our quantum fire construction QFireMini are O0, O1, O2, OOSS.Ver, OOSS.Sign, and the flame state is the purified output |η⟩ of OSS.GenQKey(1<sup>λ</sup> ). To verify an alleged flame state, QFireMini.Ver tries signing (using OSS.Sign) first the message 0, and then rewinds and tries signing the message 1, and accepts if both succeed. We note that QFireMini.Ver does not only accept |η⟩ - it actually accepts any OSS signing key |ψvk⟩, but we prove that this is not a security issue. Finally, QFireMini.Clone proceeds by simulating OSS.GenQKey, where oracle queries are simulated by forwarding them to (O0, O1, O2) and decrypting as described above. Note that we do not actually give a direct construction for QFireMini, and the scheme summarized above was for exposition. Our full scheme is a key-fire scheme for signing and is given in [Section 9.](#page-53-0)

## <span id="page-13-0"></span>2.3 Proving Security

We now give a high-level overview of our proof strategy: Our full security proof [\(Section 10\)](#page-63-0) is highly delicate and technical. Note that our formal proof is for the stronger notion of quantum key-fire, whereas in this section for ease of exposition we will discuss the case of quantum fire mini-schemes.

{14}------------------------------------------------

As argued before, to prove security, we hope to show that [Step 1] O<sup>2</sup> has a sound verification mechanism so that the receiver adversary A<sup>2</sup> will not be able to use the oracle OOSS.GenQKey (except at few points) and [Step 2] the unencrypted parts of the original oracle (OOSS.Sign, OOSS.Ver) are not leaking too much information, that is, A<sup>2</sup> is not able to produce |η⟩ when it cannot unlock oracle OOSS.GenQKey (but can access OOSS.Sign, OOSS.Ver).

We first start with the second point. To isolate [Step 2], assume that the receiver adversary A<sup>2</sup> has access to the oracles O0, O1, OOSS.Sign, OOSS.Ver, but does not have access to O<sup>2</sup> (so, no access to OOSS.GenQKey). Note that A<sup>1</sup> still has access to all oracles (O0, O1, O2, OOSS.Sign, OOSS.Ver) of quantum fire. In this case, it is still not immediately clear if A<sup>2</sup> is not able to produce |η⟩. After all, OOSS.Sign, OOSS.Ver might be leaking too much information - indeed, the trivial splitting of the oracles of OSS would set them to be the same as the full oracle OOSS.GenQKey. However, we prove that there is actually an OSS construction such that any A<sup>2</sup> that has access to only OOSS.Sign, OOSS.Ver and receives a classical message from A<sup>1</sup> provably cannot produce a signing key |ϕvk⟩!

Incompressibility We note that the above security requirement is somewhat unnatural for an OSS scheme, since for one-shot signature schemes, there is no concept of secrecy (indeed, |η⟩ is a publicly generatable state!). Thus, instead of proving the above directly, we introduce a stronger yet more natural property for OSS schemes called incompressibility.

Definition 1 (Incompressible One-Shot Signatures [\(Definitions 21](#page-35-2) and [22,](#page-36-1) informal)). A one-shot signature scheme relative to an oracle (OOSS.Sign, OOSS.Ver, OOSS.GenQKey) is said to be incompressible if it satisfies the following for any efficient adversary A1, A2.

A<sup>1</sup> gets access to OOSS.Sign, OOSS.Ver, OOSS.GenQKey, and produces a classical output L. Then, A<sup>2</sup> receives L and oracle access to only OOSS.Sign, OOSS.Ver. We require that there is a small (i.e. polynomial size) list of verification key values such that with overwhelming probability, any valid signature-verification key pair produced by any A<sup>2</sup> on input L must come from LL.

In a sense, incompressibility says that not too many signatures can be packed in to a classical leakage string. Note that a quantum message analogue of this incompressibility notion is not achievable: A<sup>1</sup> can simply send an equal superposition of exponentially many signatures.

We prove that the OSS construction of [\[SZ25\]](#page-95-3), with a careful splitting of the oracles, satisfies incompressibility. For technical reasons that will be clear later on, we also prove an incompressibility property for random oracles [\(Section 7\)](#page-46-0). We believe the notion of incompressibility and our results might be of independent interest. Our formal definitions, constructions and proofs are given in [Section 7](#page-46-0) and [Section 6.](#page-35-1) Before moving on, we note that while incompressibility is a natural property, it is not automatically satisfied by any OSS scheme and thus requires a completely new proof separate from one-shot security. For example, one can think of an OSS scheme where the signatures and verification keys are rerandomizable, or an OSS scheme where OOSS.Sign additionally outputs a signature for a random verification key. None of these properties would cause a violation of one-shot signature security, but they would trivially break incompressibility.

Solving [Step 2] Observe that with the incompressibility property, we complete [Step 2] as follows. Suppose for a contradiction that A<sup>2</sup> (with no access to O2) receives L from A<sup>1</sup> and produces a state ρ that passes QFireMini.Ver with non-negligible probability. However, we can then run A2(L) twice to obtain to two copies of ρ, and with one of them we can sign 0 to get vk1, isig<sup>1</sup> and with the other one we can sign 1 to obtain vk2, isig2. Since we said QFireMini.Ver accepts ρ with non-negligible probability, we can then conclude that (vk1, isig1) and (vk2, isig2) will both be valid 

{15}------------------------------------------------

with non-negligible probability by Jensen's inequality. However, by incompressibility property, we also know that  $vk_1$  and  $vk_2$  come from a small set, thus we can show that with non-negligible probability,  $vk_1$  and  $vk_2$  will be the same! This gives a contradiction to one-shot security, thus completing proof of [Step 2].

Oracle Encryption Security: Proving [Step 1] We essentially argued (in [Step 2]) that untelegraphability security is satisfied if  $\mathcal{A}_2$  did not have access to  $\mathcal{O}_{OSS.GenQKey}$ . Now, we consider [Step 1]: actually showing that our oracle encryption encryption ensures that this is the case. As discussed before, to do this, we want to rely on the fact that  $\mathcal{O}_2$  in some sense verifies  $|\eta\rangle$  before producing its output. However, we are trying to show that  $\mathcal{A}_2$  cannot produce  $|\eta\rangle$  to begin with! This circularity problem also persists when we try to utilize the incompressibility property: It only applies if  $\mathcal{A}_2$  does not have access to  $\mathcal{O}_{OSS.GenQKey}$ , but that's what we are trying to show to begin with!

To solve this issue and eliminate the circularity problem, we take a fine-grained approach: We consider the execution of  $\mathcal{A}_2$  query-by-query. In particular, we consider<sup>11</sup> the queries of  $\mathcal{A}_2$  to  $\mathcal{O}_0, \mathcal{O}_1, \mathcal{O}_2$  to proceed in triplets (with queries to  $\mathcal{O}_{OSS.Sign}, \mathcal{O}_{OSS.Ver}$  in between the triplets). We will slowly remove these query triplets and instead simulate them with messages from  $\mathcal{A}_1$ , and eventually we will end up with a  $\mathcal{A}'_2$  which makes no queries  $\mathcal{O}_2$ , in which case our discussion above shows untelegraphability security.

To this end, first, observe that the part of  $A_2$  until its first query to  $\mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{OSS.GenQKey}}$  is executed can actually be considered as an adversary that does not have any queries to  $\mathcal{O}_{\mathsf{OSS}.\mathsf{GenQKey}}$  (after all, by definition it does not make any queries to this oracle before its first query to this oracle). Thus, we can actually invoke the incompressibility property for this part (call it  $\mathcal{A}_2^{(1)}$ ). Due to the incompressibility property of OSS, we know that there is a small list  $\mathfrak{L}$  such that when  $\mathcal{A}'_2$  passes the verification of  $\mathcal{O}_0$  and  $\mathcal{O}_1$ , it must be for some  $vk \in \mathfrak{L}$ . Thus, essentially, we can remove the access of  $A_2$  to the regions of the random oracle  $H_0(vk||\cdot)$  and  $H_1(vk||\cdot)$  for  $vk \notin \mathfrak{L}$ . Now, we know that during its query to  $\mathcal{O}_2$ ,  $\mathcal{A}_2$  will not have made any queries to  $H_0(vk||\cdot)$ ,  $H_1(vk||\cdot)$  for  $vk \notin \mathfrak{L}$ . Then, we also observe that when  $A_2$  is querying  $\mathcal{O}_2$ , its valid queries cannot have  $vk \in \mathfrak{L}$  since otherwise we actually show that we can extract a signature for both 0 and 1 with same vk from  $A_2$ ! Thus, we can invoke the incompressibility of random oracles  $H_0, H_1$  outside the region  $vk \notin \mathfrak{L}$  to say that valid queries of  $A_2$  to  $\mathcal{O}_2$  must have z from a small set  $\mathfrak{L}'$ . Thus, before moving onto the next query, we can actually consider the outputs of  $\mathcal{O}_2$  on these z values to be part of a new leakage from  $\mathcal{A}'_1$  (who evaluates the oracle on this set and sends teh outputs), and thus remove the first query of  $A_2$  to  $\mathcal{O}_2$  altogether and simulate it! One by one, we remove the queries of  $A_2$ . Finally we show how to simulate the protocol between  $A_1$  and  $A_2$  using a single message and polynomially many queries by  $\mathcal{A}_1^{sim}$  and no queries by  $\mathcal{A}_2^{sim}$ . The full proof is technical and deals with many other challenges (e.g., showing that we can extract a signature for 0 and 1 if valid query of  $A_2$  has  $vk \in \mathfrak{L}$ ).

Another major point we swept under the rug in the discussion above is that, while we argued that we can remove oracle calls of  $\mathcal{A}_2$  and replace them with simulations using classical messages from  $\mathcal{A}'_1$ , this actually requires knowing the small sets  $\mathcal{L}, \mathcal{L}'$  on which  $\mathcal{A}_2$  can actually decrypt the oracles. However, these existential sets (whose existence are guaranteed by the incompressibility properties of OSS and random oracles) are actually not available to use directly, so we cannot simply say that  $\mathcal{A}'_1$  evaluates the oracles on these sets and sends the outputs to  $\mathcal{A}'_2$ . In general, these lists can depend on the full descriptions of the oracles and are not fully determined by the initial message L of  $\mathcal{A}_1$ , since  $\mathcal{A}_1$  can use some outputs of the oracle as a one-time pad to encode other outputs,

<span id="page-15-0"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>11</sup>This is without loss of generality

{16}------------------------------------------------

and  $A_1$  and  $A_2$  can have a special encoding in their communication protocol. As another example, these adversaries can even use global properties of the oracles (such finding a preimage for a value using Yamakawa-Zhandry [YZ22]) in their encoding process. Thus, in general we will not have access to these sets, and giving these sets directly to the adversaries might break one-shot security (which we crucially rely on multiple points), or even break incompressibility property itself(!) since it is a global information about the oracles. To solve this issue, we show an estimation procedure where  $A_1$  estimates these sets by making polynomially many queries, and then uses the estimations, which we prove makes only a small difference. We refer the reader to Section 10 for details.

### <span id="page-16-0"></span>2.4 Interactive Security

In this work, we also initiate the study of *interactive* untelegraphability security for quantum fire. We first note that the previous definitions of untelegraphability security is very limited. In fact, while we do not show this explicitly, using our techniques it is easy to construct a scheme that satisfies (1-message) untelegraphability security, but its security breaks down fully when  $A_2$  first sends a message to  $A_1$  and  $A_1$  replies afterwards. Further, interactive adversaries better capture real-life leakage attacks: For example, when the flame states encode the secret key of a PKE scheme (see *key-fire* below), the adversary  $A_2$  will choose the leakage function depending on pk, which is not captured by previous definitions of untelegraphability.

Security Game We now give an overview of the interactive security game for mini-schemes, and the case of full schemes is defined similarly (see Definition 16 for the formal definition). The challenger samples a flame state as  $sn^*$ ,  $R_{\text{flame}} \leftarrow \text{QFireMini.Spark}(1^{\lambda})$ , and sends  $sn^*$ ,  $R_{\text{flame}}$  to  $\mathcal{A}_1$ . Then,  $\mathcal{A}_1$  and  $\mathcal{A}_2$  interact over a classical channel for any number of rounds. Finally, outputs a register R', which is tested for  $sn^*$  by running QFireMini.Ver $(sn^*, R')$ .

**Proving Security** For interactive security, our construction stays the same as before, however, our proof is significantly different. Here we give a high-level overview, and but the full proof is highly delicate and technical - we refer the reader to Section 10 for details.

First, recall that our proof of untelegraphability security as discussed above crucially relied on running  $A_2$  twice on the message L from  $A_1$  to obtain two signatures, then we invoked incompressibility to obtain a contradiction to the one-shot security of OSS. However, once we move to the interactive setting, our argument completely breaks down: We can no longer run  $A_2$  twice since it has a (possibly unclonable) internal state, and incompressibility guarantees only apply to a non-interactive adversary  $(A_1, A_2)$ . To solve this, we show how to *clone* the internal state of the adversary  $A_2$  and also round-collapse the leakage protocol between  $A_1$  and  $A_2$  into a single message. To this end, we first start with our previous idea of making a fine-grained analysis of the queries of  $\mathcal{A}_2$ . We remove the queries of  $\mathcal{A}_2$  to the oracles  $\mathcal{O}_0, \mathcal{O}_1, \mathcal{O}_2$  one by one and replace them with estimated ones using the incompressibility. Finally, after removing these calls, we go further and also show how to estimate and remove the calls of  $A_2$  to all the other oracles! Once we remove these calls, we are able to show that we decrease the number of rounds of interaction by making  $\mathcal{A}_1$  simulate the first round, and then telling  $\mathcal{A}_2$  the outcome of the first round, after which  $\mathcal{A}_2$ locally prepares its internal state conditioned on that outcome/transcript so far! While normally it requires exponential many trials to correctly condition, since we have removed the queries of  $A_2$ (which are instead being simulated by simply reading from the database created by  $A_1$ ), we can actually afford to do this procedure. Finally once we collapse down to a single message from  $A_1$ to  $A_2$  with no actual oracle calls from  $A_2$ , we can also clone the internal state of  $A_2$ . Thus, we

{17}------------------------------------------------

are able to apply our previous argument based on one-shot security. The full proof deals with the challenges in making these arguments actually work, and is given in [Section 10.](#page-63-0)

## <span id="page-17-0"></span>2.5 Quantum Key-Fire

In this work, we also introduce a new notion called quantum key-fire, where the flame states are functional and serve as a key. We give an overview, and the formal definitions can be found in [Sec](#page-32-0)[tion 5.3.](#page-32-0) A quantum key-fire scheme consists of three algorithms: QKeyFire.Protect, QKeyFire.Eval, QKeyFire.Clone. For a function f (modeled as a circuit), Protect(1<sup>λ</sup> , f) outputs a flame state |ψpp,f ⟩ along with public parameters pp. QKeyFire.Eval(pp, |ψpp,f ⟩, x) evaluates the encoded functionality at x. Finally, QKeyFire.Clone(pp, |ψpp,f ⟩) clones the flame state. For correctness, we require that Eval(pp, |ψpp,f ⟩, x) outputs f(x), and that Clone(pp, |ψpp,f ⟩) outputs |ψpp,f ⟩ ⊗ |ψpp,f ⟩. For security, we require that after an adversary obtains unbounded classical leakage on |ψpp,f ⟩ adaptively over any number of rounds, it still cannot correctly evaluate f at a challenge x ∗ [\(Definition 8\)](#page-26-1).

Through known techniques ([\[CLLZ21,](#page-94-0) [ALL](#page-93-5)+21, [C¸ G25\]](#page-93-1)), it is easy to see that quantum keyfire for signing [\(Definition 18\)](#page-33-1) implies quantum key-fire for any unlearnable functionality (in the classical oracle model). Thus, in the rest of the overview, we mainly consider quantum key-fire for signing. In the security game for this primitive [\(Definition 19\)](#page-33-2), the adversary A receives LOCC leakage on the signing key state Rflame: For any number of rounds, it (adaptively) specifies a quantum circuit with classical output as a leakage function, which is then applied to Rflame and the outcome is sent to A. After the leakage, it is asked to sign a random message m<sup>∗</sup> . We require that any efficient adversary can win this game with negligible probability. Note that equivalently, this game can also be modeled as two adversaries A1, A<sup>2</sup> interacting over a classical channel where A<sup>1</sup> receives Rflame and after the interaction, A<sup>2</sup> receives the challenge m<sup>∗</sup> and produces a signature (see [Definition 24](#page-51-2) for this formalization).

Construction and Proving Security We briefly discuss our construction (we refer the reader to [Section 9](#page-53-0) for our full construction). The starting point of our construction is our key-fire construction, but with the addition of a new oracle that verifies the flame states and outputs shares of a random oracle evaluation Hsig(m) which serves a signature on m. In the proof, using the techniques discussed before, we show that we can round-collapse the leakage protocol down to a single message, and make the internal state of A<sup>2</sup> clonable. At this point, we argue (thanks to incompressibility) that running two copies of A<sup>2</sup> will query the evaluation oracle with the same OSS verification key, and we will be able to extract two OSS signatures to obtain a violation of the one-shot security. The full proof is highly delicate and technical - we refer the reader to [Section 10.](#page-63-0)

### <span id="page-17-1"></span>2.6 Related Work

Nehoran and Zhandry ([\[NZ24\]](#page-95-1)) did not formally define quantum fire, however, it introduced the complexity class clonableQMA and showed a quantum oracle separation between clonableQMA and QCMA. Note that clonableQMA sits between QMA and QCMA, thus a separation between clonableQMA and QCMA also leads to a separation between QMA and QCMA (note that such a separation was already known in the quantum oracle model). They also mentioned possible cryptographic applications, thus hinting at quantum fire. Indeed, their separation is also implicitly a quantum fire construction relative to the same quantum oracle, as observed by [\[BNZ25\]](#page-93-4). Unfortunately their techniques bake cloning into the oracle itself, and thus it uses an inherently inefficient quantum oracle and there is no hope even instantiating it even heuristically.

{18}------------------------------------------------

Bostanci, Nehoran, Zhandry ([\[BNZ25\]](#page-93-4)) formalized the notion of quantum fire. Then, they gave a candidate construction based on group actions, and proved its cloning correctness. However, even in the classical oracle model, they only conjectured its security and could not give a proof of security or argue security. In fact, as discussed before, they even claimed that any provably secure quantum fire scheme in the classical oracle model would likely lead to a classical oracle separation between QMA and QCMA, which is one of the biggest questions in quantum cryptography that has been open since 2007. Indeed, since [\[NZ24\]](#page-95-1) separates both clonableQMA and QCMA, and at the same time their separation is implicitly a quantum fire construction, it is an interesting question if there is any connection and if our techniques could be useful.

We also discuss a follow-up work, [\[HV25\]](#page-94-8). The initial version of our paper on arXiV contained a small gap in only in the last hybrid of our quantum key-fire proof (not quantum-fire proof), which was communicated to us by the authors of [\[HV25\]](#page-94-8) and at the same time they put their paper online. Within 24 hours of receiving their communication, we fixed the small gap in our proof and let them know. Current version of our paper does not have this gap, however, we include a discussion in [Section B](#page-98-0) for historical reference.

For leakage-resilience, [\[C¸ GLZR24\]](#page-94-1) introduced the notion of unbounded leakage-resilience and LOCC leakage-resilience for various cryptographic primitives such as public-key encryption and digital signatures. They also showed that the previous unclonable/copy-protected constructions of [\[CLLZ21,](#page-94-0) [LLQZ22\]](#page-94-9) also satisfy LOCC leakage-resilience. Therefore, they also asked the question of whether leakage-resilience security implies copy-protection security.

{19}------------------------------------------------

## <span id="page-19-0"></span>3 Preliminaries

In this section, we highlight some of our notation and conventions, and recall some basics about classical cryptography and quantum information.

### <span id="page-19-1"></span>3.1 Notation and Conventions

We refer the reader to [Gol01, Gol04] for a preliminary on cryptography and to [NC10] for a preliminary on quantum information and computation. We follow the notations and conventions commonly used in (quantum) cryptography, theoretical computer science and mathematics. In this section, we non-exhaustively highlight some of them. All of our conventions are implicitly followed unless otherwise specified, e.g. our cryptographic assumptions are always post-quantum even though most of the time we will not write this explicitly, and if there is a rare occasion where we assume classical security only, we will specify explicitly.

**Functions** For a function  $f: \mathbb{N}^+ \to \mathbb{N}^+$ , we will say that it is an efficiently computable function if it is polynomially bounded, that is, if there is some  $n_0 \in \mathbb{N}^+$  and  $c \in \mathbb{R}^+$  such that  $f(n) \leq n^c$  for all  $n > n_0$ . Sometimes we will say polynomial to also mean polynomially bounded instead (e.g. when we say polynomial, it means  $O(\operatorname{poly}(\lambda))$ , which includes, say,  $\sqrt{n}$ ) - the precise meaning will be clear from context.  $f(\lambda)$  is said to be negligible if for any  $c \in \mathbb{N}^+$ , there is  $n_0 \in \mathbb{N}^+$  such that  $f(\lambda) \leq \frac{1}{n^c}$  for all  $n \geq n_0$ .  $f(\lambda)$  is said to superlogarithmic if  $f = \omega(\log(\lambda))$  or equivalently, if  $2^{-f(\lambda)}$  is negligible.

When we describe a function, such as a polynomial, if no input is mentioned, it will implicitly be the security parameter  $\lambda$ .

Classical and Quantum Information and Oracles Variables written in lower-case, such as pk, sk, are classical objects. We write R to mean a quantum register, which keeps a quantum state that will evolve when the register is acted on, and it can be entangled with other registers.

We write  $R \leftarrow \rho$  to mean that the register R is initialized with a sample from the quantum distribution (i.e. mixed state)  $\rho$ . For simplicity, we will usually write non-normalized versions of the quantum states, however, they are always implicitly normalized.

We write  $\mathcal{O}$  to denote a classical oracle, and write  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathcal{O}}$  to denote an algorithm that has quantum query-access to  $\mathcal{O}$ . Similarly, for unitary oracles we write U and  $\mathcal{A}^{U}$ . All adversaries always have quantum access to all of the oracles.

**Distributions** When S is a set, we write  $x \leftarrow S$  to mean that x is sampled uniformly at random from S. When  $\mathcal{D}$  is a distribution, we write  $x \leftarrow \mathcal{D}$  to mean that x is sampled from  $\mathcal{D}$ . Finally, we write  $x \leftarrow \mathcal{B}(R)$  or  $x \leftarrow \mathcal{B}(a)$  to mean that x is a sample as output by the (quantum or classical randomized) algorithm  $\mathcal{B}$  run on the quantum input register R or classical input a. We use similar notation for quantum registers, e.g.,  $R \leftarrow \mathcal{B}(a)$ .

Adversaries and Constructions/Algorithms We write QPT to mean quantum polynomial time and PPT to mean probabilistic (classical) polynomial time. We say query-bounded to mean that the algorithm (e.g. construction, adversary) makes polynomially many queries to the oracle, but it can possibly take unbounded time outside the queries. All of these are polynomial in the security parameter  $\lambda$ . Note that for QPT and PPT, this also means that the inputs to these algorithms are also of polynomial size in  $\lambda$ .

{20}------------------------------------------------

We say *efficient* to mean QPT or PPT (will be clear from context) in the plain model, or query-bounded in the oracle model. For constructions, efficient means QPT or PPT, even in the oracle model (though the oracles themselves might not be efficiently sampleable or implementable)

In the plain model, adversaries will be implicitly QPT. In the oracle security model, adversaries will be implicitly query-bounded. An admissible adversary means an adversary that conforms to the (implicit) requirements of the security game (e.g. polynomial time or query bounded, has the correct interaction model and has correct input-output format and so on). All of our cryptographical assumptions are post-quantum, e.g., one-way functions means post-quantum secure one-way functions. Adversaries are stateful uniform QPT.

When we say that a primitive is subexponentially secure, we mean that any polynomial time adversary has subexponentially small advantage. Sometimes we require security against subexponential time adversaries, in which case we will specify this explicitly.

All algorithms of a cryptographic scheme are uniform (stateless) PPT or QPT (will be clear from context).

Security Games A security game is a binary random variable which is the outcome of an experiment between a *challenger* and an adversary. For a security game, we say that the adversary has won if the experiment output is 1, and otherwise we say that the adversary has lost. When we say that an adversary has negligible advantage, we mean that the probability of the adversary winning (or distinguishing, depending on context) is upper bounded by a negligible function of  $\lambda$ .

**Domains** We write  $\mathcal{M}$  to denote the message space for various cryptographic primitives, and it will be equal to  $\{0,1\}^{p(\lambda)}$  for some polynomial  $p(\cdot)$  (will be clear from context).

### <span id="page-20-0"></span>3.2 Pseudorandom Functions

In this section, we recall pseudorandom functions (PRF) and puncturable PRFs.

**Definition 2** (Pseudorandom Functions). A pseudorandom function (PRF) scheme PRF is a family of functions  $\{F: \{0,1\}^{c(\lambda)} \times \{0,1\}^{m(\lambda)} \to \{0,1\}^{n(\lambda)}\}_{\lambda \in \mathbb{N}^+}$  along with the following efficient algorithms.

- PRF.Gen(1 $^{\lambda}$ ): Takes in the unary representation of the security parameter and outputs a key K in  $\{0,1\}^{c(\lambda)}$ .
- PRF.Eval(K,x): Takes in a key  $K \in \{0,1\}^{c(\lambda)}$  and an input  $x \in \{0,1\}^{m(\lambda)}$ , outputs an evaluation of the PRF in  $\{0,1\}^{n(\lambda)}$ .

We require the following.

Correctness.

$$\Pr \big[ \forall x \; \mathsf{PRF}.\mathsf{Eval}(K,x) = F(K,x) : \; K \leftarrow \mathsf{PRF}.\mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda}) \; \big] = 1.$$

**Security** We require that for any QPT adversary A,

$$\left|\Pr_{H\leftarrow \mathbf{H}}[\mathcal{A}^H(1^\lambda)=1]-\Pr_{K\leftarrow \mathsf{PRF}.\mathsf{Gen}(1^\lambda)}[\mathcal{A}^{F(K,\cdot)}(1^\lambda)=1]\right|\leq \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

where H is the uniform distribution over the functions  $\{0,1\}^{m(\lambda)} \to \{0,1\}^{n(\lambda)}$ .

{21}------------------------------------------------

We will usually write FK(x) or F(K, x) in algorithms and this will implicitly mean PRF.Eval(K, x).

Definition 3 (Puncturable Pseudorandom Functions [\[SW14\]](#page-95-8)). A puncturable pseudorandom function (PRF) scheme PRF is a pseudorandom function scheme along with the following additional efficient algorithm.

• PRF.Puncture(K, S) : Takes as input a key K ∈ {0, 1} <sup>c</sup>(λ) and a predicate circuit C : {0, 1} <sup>m</sup>(λ) → {0, 1}, outputs a punctured key.

We require the following.

Punctured Correctness. For all efficient distributions D(1<sup>λ</sup> ) over predicate circuits, we require

$$\Pr\left[\forall x \in \{0,1\}^{m(\lambda)}C(x) = 1 \implies \mathsf{PRF}.\mathsf{Eval}(K_C,x) = F(K,x) : \begin{array}{c} C \leftarrow \mathcal{D}(1^{\lambda}) \\ K \leftarrow \mathsf{PRF}.\mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda}) \\ K_C \leftarrow \mathsf{PRF}.\mathsf{Puncture}(K,C) \end{array}\right] = 1.$$

Puncturing Security We require that any QPT adversary A wins the following game with probability at most 1/2 + negl(λ).

- 1. The adversary A outputs a predicate circuit C.
- 2. The challenger samples K ← PRF.Setup(1<sup>λ</sup> ) and K<sup>C</sup> ← PRF.Puncture(K, C), and submits K<sup>C</sup> to the adversary A.
- 3. The adversary A outputs an input x such that C(x) = 0.
- 4. The challenger samples b ← {0, 1}. If b = 0, the challenger submits F(K, x) to the adversary. Otherwise, it submits y to the adversary where y ← {0, 1} n(λ) .
- 5. The adversary outputs a guess b ′ and we say that the adversary has won if b ′ = b.

It is easy to see that any puncturable PRF scheme that satisfies puncturing security also satisfies usual PRF security.

Theorem 5 ([\[SW14,](#page-95-8) [Zha12\]](#page-95-9)). Let n(·), m(·) be polynomially bounded.

- If (post-quantum) one-way functions exist, then there exists a (post-quantum) puncturable PRF with input space {0, 1} <sup>m</sup>(λ) and output space {0, 1} n(λ) .
- If subexponentially-secure (post-quantum) one-way functions exist, then for any c > 0, there exists a (post-quantum) 2 −λ c -secure[12](#page-21-0) puncturable PRF with input space {0, 1} <sup>m</sup>(λ) and output space {0, 1} n(λ) .

<span id="page-21-0"></span><sup>12</sup>While the original results are for negligible security against polynomial time adversaries, it is easy to see that they carry over to subexponential security. Further, by scaling the security parameter by a polynomial and simple input/output conversions, subexponentially secure (for any exponent c ′ ) one-way functions is sufficient to construct for any c a puncturable PRF that is 2<sup>−</sup><sup>λ</sup> c -secure.

{22}------------------------------------------------

## <span id="page-22-0"></span>3.3 Digital Signature Schemes

<span id="page-22-2"></span>In this section we recall the definition of signatures schemes.

Definition 4. A digital signature scheme with message space M consists of the following efficient algorithms that satisfy the correctness and security guarantees below.

- Setup(1<sup>λ</sup> ) : Takes the unary representation of the security parameter and outputs a signing key sk and a verification key vk.
- Sign(sk, m) : Takes a signing key sk and a message m ∈ M, returns a signature for m.
- Ver(vk, m, sig) : Takes the public verification key vk, a message m ∈ M and an alleged signature sig for m, outputs 1 if s is a valid signature for m.

Correctness We require the following for all messages m ∈ M.

$$\Pr \left[ \mathsf{Ver}(vk, m, s) = 1 : \begin{array}{c} sk, vk \leftarrow \mathsf{Setup}(1^{\lambda}) \\ s \leftarrow \mathsf{Sign}(sk, m) \end{array} \right] = 1.$$

Adaptive existential-unforgeability security under chosen message attack (EUF-CMA) We require that any QPT adversary A wins the following game with negligible probability.

- 1. The challenger samples the keys sk, vk ← Setup(1).
- 2. The adversary A receives vk, and interacts with the signing oracle by sending classical messages m ∈ M and receiving the corresponding signatures that is computed by the challenger as sig ← Sign(sk, m).
- 3. The adversary A outputs a message m<sup>∗</sup> ∈ M that it has not queried the oracle with and a forged signature sig<sup>∗</sup> .
- 4. The challenger outputs 1 if and only if Ver(vk, m<sup>∗</sup> , sig<sup>∗</sup> ) = 1.

If the adversary A is required to output the message m<sup>∗</sup> before receiving vk, we call it selective EUF-CMA security.

### <span id="page-22-1"></span>3.4 Indistinguishability Obfuscation

In this section, we recall indistinguishability obfuscation (iO).

Definition 5 (Indistinguishability Obfuscation). An indistinguishability obfuscation (iO) scheme for a class of circuits C = {Cλ}<sup>λ</sup> is an efficient algorithm iO that satisfies the following.

Correctness. For all λ ∈ N <sup>+</sup>, C ∈ C<sup>λ</sup> and all inputs x to C,

$$\Pr\left[\tilde{C}(x) = C(x) : \tilde{C} \leftarrow i\mathcal{O}(1^{\lambda}, C)\right] = 1.$$

{23}------------------------------------------------

**Security.** Let  $\mathcal{B}$  be any QPT algorithm that outputs two circuits  $C_0, C_1 \in \mathcal{C}$  of the same size, along with quantum auxiliary information  $\mathsf{R}_{\mathsf{aux}}$ , such that  $\Pr\left[\forall x \ C_0(x) = C_1(x) : (C_0, C_1, \mathsf{R}_{\mathsf{aux}}) \leftarrow \mathcal{B}(1^{\lambda})\right] \geq 1 - \mathsf{negl}(\lambda)$ . Then, for any QPT adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ ,
